confirm users understand when they are in weaker metadata conditions;

oversharing misuse testing
verify the UI does not make broad disclosure easier than narrow proofs;

recovery comprehension testing
confirm users understand the difference between help and custody;

weak-connectivity testing
validate degraded-mode behavior on mobile.

If these fail, the product layer must be redesigned before launch.

29. Formal discard triggers

This product architecture is rejected if any of the following becomes true:

ordinary users cannot tell which layer they are using;

the easiest UX path is effectively custodial;

privacy degradation is silent;

receipts and invoice proofs are harder to use than full-history exports;

business usability requires broad transparency;

or recovery convenience quietly replaces self-sovereignty.

30. Resulting decisions

This specification closes these questions:

product UX must expose trust and privacy boundaries honestly;

the wallet must distinguish base, fast, and stable layers clearly;

strong and degraded privacy states must be visible;

receipts, invoices, refunds, and recurring payments are first-class flows;

recovery must be explicit about trust trade-offs;

and the narrowest adequate disclosure should be the easiest product path.

31. What remains open

This specification leaves open:

the exact wallet information architecture,

the exact disclosure UX patterns,

the exact merchant API surface,

the exact recovery policy templates,

and the exact business accounting export format.

The next correct deliverable is:

Specification 11 — Integrated Failure Model, Kill Criteria, Launch Gates, and Program Sequence

continue
Thought for a few seconds
Specification 11 — Integrated Failure Model, Kill Criteria, Launch Gates, and Program Sequence
1. Purpose

This specification defines how the project decides:

what counts as success,

what counts as unacceptable failure,

when to stop,

when to simplify,

and when the architecture is mature enough to move from concept to implementation to launch.

It answers this question:

how do we prevent this program from becoming another elegant theory that accumulates complexity, survives on optimism, and launches before its contradictions are actually resolved?

This document covers:

integrated failure modes,

kill criteria,

downgrade criteria,

launch gates,

validation sequence,

and execution order across the entire program.

It does not redefine:

consensus,

privacy model,

stable-layer economics,

governance boundaries,

or product invariants.

2. Design thesis

A project like this does not usually die because one theorem is wrong.

It dies because:

several “small” unresolved problems interact,

complexity becomes socially unreviewable,

product pressure erodes the architecture,

and nobody writes down the conditions under which the project should stop or radically simplify.

So this specification imposes a brutal rule:

every subsystem must justify its existence under adversarial testing, and the architecture must define in advance which failures are survivable, which require simplification, and which require stopping the program entirely.

This is not pessimism.
It is anti-self-deception.

3. Scope

This specification covers:

whole-system risk integration,

hard failure categories,

subsystem interaction risks,

downgrade and simplification rules,

launch readiness gates,

and the execution sequence for the program.

It also defines the difference between:

prototype success,

research success,

launch success,

and real-world survival.

4. Non-goals

This specification does not promise:

certainty,

zero unknowns,

or a guaranteed path to mass adoption.

It also does not try to preserve sunk cost.

If the architecture fails its own criteria, the correct response may be:

cut scope,

replace a subsystem,

or kill the project.

5. Failure philosophy

The program distinguishes three classes of failure.

5.1 Local failure

A subsystem fails but can be replaced or isolated without destroying the architecture.

Examples:

one wallet UX model fails,

one relay strategy fails,

one proof format is too heavy,

one merchant API is unusable.

5.2 Structural failure

A subsystem fails in a way that breaks a core architectural claim and forces redesign.

Examples:

light clients cannot avoid catastrophic privacy leakage,

blockDAG ordering becomes too complex to review independently,

stable-layer isolation fails,

mining centralization becomes unavoidable too early.

5.3 Constitutional failure

A failure breaks a non-negotiable invariant and invalidates the architecture as currently conceived.

Examples:

privacy by default becomes practically impossible,

consensus capture resistance collapses,

supply correctness cannot be audited with confidence,

legal interoperability only works via transparency by default,

productization requires hidden custody as the main path.

6. Integrated risk map

The architecture must be evaluated not only by subsystem correctness, but by subsystem interaction.

The highest-risk interaction zones are these.

6.1 Consensus × private state

Risk:

ordering ambiguity,

nullifier conflict ambiguity,

reorg complexity,

verifier burden explosion.

6.2 Private state × light clients

Risk:

recipient discovery leaks,

remote scanning dependence,

note-state ambiguity,

impractical mobile verification.

6.3 Network privacy × product UX

Risk:

privacy defaults become too slow,

users normalize degraded modes,

wallet vendors weaken network protections for convenience.

6.4 Stable layer × governance

Risk:

treasury control becomes political control,

stability pressure mutates base-layer rules.

6.5 Business interoperability × privacy

Risk:

disclosure tooling becomes broad transparency,

merchants and auditors demand full-history exports.

6.6 Issuance × mining decentralization

Risk:

fair launch still concentrates,

security budget favors industrial operators,

small miners become symbolic too early.

6.7 Recovery UX × sovereignty

Risk:

recovery helpers become de facto custodians,

social recovery drifts into service custody.

The program must treat these interaction zones as primary review surfaces.

7. Non-negotiable invariants

If any of these fail, the current architecture is invalid.

privacy by default at the base layer;

no stake-based or visible-validator sovereignty at the base validity layer;

publicly auditable aggregate supply correctness;

base / stable separation;

selective disclosure, not transparency-by-default legal interoperability;

light-client path that is not operational privacy suicide;

product layer that does not quietly replace sovereignty with custody.

These are constitutional constraints, not stretch goals.

8. Kill criteria

The program must stop or be fundamentally restarted if any of the following become true.

8.1 Kill criterion A — privacy cannot survive practical use

If the only workable mobile or light-client model reveals near-complete wallet activity to providers, the architecture fails.

8.2 Kill criterion B — blockDAG/private-state interaction becomes unreviewably complex

If independent teams cannot confidently implement and verify canonical ordering plus private-state updates, the design fails.

8.3 Kill criterion C — supply correctness cannot be audited with high confidence

If hidden-value conservation or issuance accounting cannot be independently verified, the design fails.

8.4 Kill criterion D — base security depends on oligarchic control too early

If the chosen PoW and issuance combination leads to rapid unavoidable consensus concentration, the design fails.

8.5 Kill criterion E — stability requires hidden central-bank behavior

If the stable layer can only function by making a treasury or issuer the true sovereign center, the architecture fails.

8.6 Kill criterion F — business usability requires broad transparency

If real business operations only become viable through exposing broad histories and balances, the design fails.

8.7 Kill criterion G — the easiest consumer path is effectively custodial

If real adoption requires default hidden custodians, then the system has failed its own purpose.

9. Downgrade criteria

Some failures do not require killing the project.
They require reducing ambition.

9.1 Downgrade from blockDAG to linear chain

Trigger:

blockDAG ordering remains too complex,

light-client proofs become too heavy,

or reorg behavior becomes too confusing.

Response:

preserve PoW and privacy model,

downgrade ledger geometry.

9.2 Downgrade stable layer ambition

Trigger:

stable unit cannot avoid unacceptable oracle, treasury, or governance centralization.

Response:

postpone full stable layer,

launch with base asset + limited fast payment layer only.

9.3 Downgrade disclosure scope

Trigger:

some proof classes are too linkable or too easy to misuse.

Response:

launch with narrower disclosure primitives only.

9.4 Downgrade recovery features

Trigger:

recovery models drift too strongly toward custody.

Response:

ship stricter self-custody and threshold models first.

9.5 Downgrade merchant feature surface

Trigger:

full recurring-payment or refund machinery introduces too much abuse or oversharing risk.

Response:

ship simpler merchant flows first.

Downgrading is acceptable.
Pretending unresolved complexity is solved is not.

10. Anti-complexity rule

This project is unusually vulnerable to complexity collapse.

So it adopts this rule:

if a subsystem improves one target property but makes the total architecture materially less reviewable, less implementable, or less socially understandable, it must justify itself against a simplification alternative.

This means:

elegance is not enough,

novelty is not enough,

and “technically possible” is not enough.

Every subsystem must beat the simpler fallback in total system fitness.

11. Launch philosophy

There are four distinct levels of “done.”

11.1 Research done

The architecture is coherent enough to justify prototyping.

11.2 Prototype done

Core components work under controlled conditions.

11.3 Launch done

The system passes its launch gates and can be exposed to adversarial real use.

11.4 Survival done

The system remains usable and non-captured after real-world pressure.

The program must never confuse one level with another.

12. Launch gates

A mainnet or public adversarial launch is blocked unless all launch gates are passed.

Gate 1 — Consensus gate

Required:

deterministic ordering behavior,

stable reorg handling,

comprehensible finality policy,

adversarial testing for selfish mining / withholding / conflict resolution.

Gate 2 — Private-state gate

Required:

sound conservation proofs,

nullifier conflict soundness,

aggregate supply auditability,

and state-root determinism across independent implementations.

Gate 3 — Light-client gate

Required:

a viable mobile-capable client model,

local verification of meaningful proofs,

no catastrophic query leakage,

and acceptable bandwidth / battery cost.

Gate 4 — Network privacy gate

Required:

origin inference materially reduced,

spy-node clustering materially reduced,

degraded privacy modes explicit,

and multi-provider operation validated.

Gate 5 — Mining and issuance gate

Required:

acceptable attack-cost model,

no immediate consensus oligarchy,

meaningful small-operator participation window,

and a defensible security budget.

Gate 6 — Governance gate

Required:

emergency drills completed,

multi-implementation conformance working,

activation classes defined,

and no single-team protocol monopoly.

Gate 7 — Product gate

Required:

normal users can use the wallet without constant catastrophic mistakes,

merchants can invoice, receive, and refund,

and users understand privacy modes and finality states.

Gate 8 — Disclosure gate

Required:

narrow proofs work,

common proof compositions do not trivially recreate full financial graphs,

and business/accounting flows do not force broad transparency.

Gate 9 — Stable-layer gate

Required if stable layer launches with the system:

stress-tested collateral behavior,

explicit depeg states,

aggregate solvency visibility,

and base-layer isolation under failure.

If the stable layer does not pass, it does not launch.

13. Pre-launch sequence

The implementation sequence must be staged.

Stage 0 — formal architecture freeze

Freeze invariants, subsystem boundaries, and downgrade rules.

Stage 1 — consensus prototype

Prototype ordering, conflict resolution, and reorg behavior.

Stage 2 — private-state prototype

Prototype notes, commitments, nullifiers, and supply accounting.

Stage 3 — light-client prototype

Prove that non-suicidal mobile/private use is possible.

Stage 4 — network privacy prototype

Test relay behavior, correlation resistance, and degraded modes.

Stage 5 — product skeleton

Wallet, merchant flows, receipts, and failure-state UX.

Stage 6 — multi-implementation testbed

Independent implementations must agree before public launch.

Stage 7 — adversarial integrated testnet

All major subsystems under hostile conditions together.

Stage 8 — limited-scope public deployment

Only after integrated gates pass.

This order is intentional.
Productization cannot be allowed to outrun core validity and privacy.

14. Testnet sequence

The program should run more than one testnet phase.

14.1 Cryptography and state testnet

Focus:

proof validity,

nullifiers,

commitments,

state roots.

14.2 Consensus and reorg testnet

Focus:

DAG or chain behavior,

conflict resolution,

attack simulations.

14.3 Light-client and network-privacy testnet

Focus:

wallet sync,

provider model,

relay behavior,

mobile viability.

14.4 Product and business-flow testnet

Focus:

invoices,

receipts,

refunds,

payroll flows,

disclosure bundles.

14.5 Integrated adversarial testnet

Focus:

everything at once,

with explicit hostile operators and failure injection.

15. Integrated metrics

The program must not over-focus on vanity metrics.

The most important metrics are these.

15.1 Consensus capture cost

How expensive is it to distort history or censor meaningfully?

15.2 Practical privacy quality

How much can providers, relays, or hostile peers infer in practice?

15.3 Light-client leakage

How much wallet activity is reconstructable by realistic adversaries?

15.4 Supply-audit confidence

How confidently can independent teams validate aggregate correctness?

15.5 Miner concentration

How quickly does the security model centralize?

15.6 Product correctness

How often do real users misinterpret mode, finality, or trust?

15.7 Oversharing risk

How often do users or businesses disclose more than required?

15.8 Stable-layer fragility

How often does the stable unit enter stress or depeg states under modeled conditions?

These matter more than raw TPS or headline node counts.

16. Integrated failure drills

Before any public launch, the program must run explicit drills for at least:

inflation bug scenario,

nullifier conflict bug,

state-root divergence,

blockDAG reorg confusion,

relay collapse,

wallet-provider deanonymization incident,

stable-layer collateral crash,

recovery-provider compromise,

and emergency upgrade coordination.

A system that cannot rehearse failure is not ready to meet it.

17. Red-team requirement

The program must include structured red-team work.

Red-team goals:

break privacy assumptions,

break user understanding,

break merchant workflows,

exploit recovery confusion,

exploit disclosure composition,

exploit mining concentration assumptions.

The red team is not there to “improve confidence.”
It is there to find what the main team wants to rationalize away.

18. Documentation boundary

The program must maintain three distinct documentation classes.

A. constitutional documents

Invariants, kill criteria, boundaries.

B. technical specifications

Consensus, private state, network, disclosure, stable layer, issuance, governance.

C. operational manuals

Incident response, wallet safety, merchant setup, recovery procedures.

These must not blur into one another.

If an operational manual starts redefining constitutional limits, the process is already compromised.

19. Go / no-go decision model

A launch decision requires:

all mandatory gates passed for included subsystems,

no active constitutional failure,

no unresolved structural failure without an accepted downgrade plan,

and an explicit written statement of remaining known risks.

“No critical bugs found lately” is not a launch criterion.

20. Post-launch survival gates

Launch is not the end of validation.

The system must define post-launch survival gates such as:

no catastrophic privacy regression under observed use,

no hidden mining concentration spike beyond acceptable band,

no light-client provider monoculture,

no disclosure default drift toward oversharing,

no treasury/governance creep from upper layers.

If those trends appear, the project must:

intervene,

simplify,

or even sunset parts of the system.

21. Sunset and rollback philosophy

The architecture must admit an uncomfortable truth:

some upper layers may deserve to be paused, removed, or sunset if they become too dangerous.

Likely candidates for partial rollback if needed:

the stable layer,

aggressive disclosure tooling,

convenience-heavy recovery modes,

or blockDAG if it proves unmanageable.

The base layer should be the hardest part to replace.
Upper layers must be more disposable.

22. Program sequence summary

The recommended execution order is:

freeze invariants and kill criteria;

lock consensus and private-state feasibility;

prove light-client viability;

prove network privacy viability;

build minimal wallet and merchant flows;

validate multi-implementation compatibility;

run adversarial integrated testnets;

only then consider stable layer and wider productization;

launch in the narrowest viable form first.

This is deliberate.

The narrowest viable architecture that survives is better than the richest architecture that only survives on paper.

23. Failure modes

This integrated program model openly admits these risks.

23.1 scope inflation

The team keeps adding features before core viability is proven.

23.2 sunk-cost blindness

A broken subsystem survives because too much time was invested.

23.3 product capture

Convenience pressure silently redefines the architecture.

23.4 complexity priesthood

Only a tiny set of experts can still reason about the full system.

23.5 gate erosion

Launch gates get softened because deadlines or excitement take over.

23.6 false victory

A testnet or prototype milestone gets mistaken for real-world readiness.

23.7 stable-layer gravity

The stable unit becomes the real system culturally, even if the base is technically sovereign.

24. Formal discard triggers

The integrated program is rejected or reset if any of the following becomes true:

core invariants are being reinterpreted to save feature ambitions;

launch gates are weakened instead of met;

simplification paths are refused for prestige reasons;

no viable non-suicidal light-client path exists;

the only practical product path is custodial;

or the architecture becomes too complex for independent review across consensus, privacy, and client behavior.

25. Resulting decisions

This specification closes these questions:

the program has explicit kill criteria;

downgrade paths are legitimate and expected;

launch requires subsystem-specific gates, not general optimism;

productization cannot outrun core validity and privacy;

upper layers are more disposable than the base layer;

and the narrowest viable launch is preferred over the richest speculative launch.

26. What remains open

This specification leaves open:

the exact threshold values for key metrics,

the exact composition of the red team,

the exact public launch sequencing,

and the exact post-launch alert thresholds.

At this point, the formal architecture set is complete enough to move into one of two next directions:

Option A: build a Master Index + dependency map of all 11 specifications so the program can be executed as a structured roadmap.

Option B: convert the architecture into a technical whitepaper draft with unified terminology, assumptions, and subsystem diagrams.

make both, A and B

I’m packaging the architecture two ways now: first as an execution map with dependencies and critical path, then as a unified whitepaper draft with consistent terminology and section flow. The first is for building; the second is for thinking, review, and eventually communicating the system coherently.

Thought for 4s

A2. Dependency graph
Tier 0 — immutable starting points

Everything depends on these:

C-01 PRD Maestro Conceptual

C-02 Integrated Failure Model

If these are unstable, all lower tiers are unstable.

Tier 1 — base validity

These must be solved before serious product or business design:

P-01 Consensus and Double-Spend

P-03 Private Transaction Model

P-04 State Commitments and Light Client

Why:

without consensus, there is no canonical history;

without private transaction correctness, there is no sound money;

without state and light-client viability, there is no realistic user path.

Tier 2 — security realism

These refine whether Tier 1 survives real-world pressure:

P-02 PoW Function and Mining Decentralization

P-05 Network Privacy and Mobile Operation

Why:

the system can be mathematically valid and still operationally captured through mining or metadata.

Tier 3 — economic and legal extensions

These should not be finalized until Tier 1 and Tier 2 are viable:

U-01 Selective Disclosure

U-02 Stable Spending Unit

U-03 Issuance and Security Budget

Why:

disclosure assumes the base can generate narrow proofs safely;

the stable unit assumes the base can survive independently;

issuance must align with actual mining and security, not aspiration.

Tier 4 — governance and productization

These depend on the shape of the lower layers:

G-01 Governance and Upgrade Control

X-01 Productization and Merchant Flows

Why:

governance depends on what kinds of changes are possible;

product depends on what can safely be abstracted.

Tier 5 — synthesis

These depend on all prior layers:

S-01 Master Index + Dependency Map

S-02 Technical Whitepaper Draft

If this path fails, there is no credible project.

G-01 Governance and Upgrade Control

If this fails, the project may launch and then die by capture.

A5. Blockers map
Blocker group B1 — base viability blockers

These block everything below them:

canonical ordering ambiguity in blockDAG;

hidden-value conservation not independently auditable;

light clients requiring catastrophic privacy leakage;

state-root determinism not reproducible across independent implementations.

Blocker group B2 — survival blockers

These block real deployment:

mining centralization too rapid;

network privacy too weak under realistic observation;

degraded mobile privacy too frequent or too silent;

provider monoculture required for wallet operation.

Blocker group B3 — usability blockers

These block meaningful adoption:

users cannot distinguish layers;

merchant flows require broad exports instead of narrow receipts;

recovery becomes effectively custodial;

disclosure composition recreates full financial graphs too easily.

Blocker group B4 — political/economic blockers

These block system coherence:

stable layer becomes hidden monetary sovereign;

bootstrap reserve becomes protocol power center;

governance can casually alter constitutional rules;

legal interoperability only works through transparency-by-default.

A6. Simplification map

If a subsystem fails, do this instead of rationalizing.

If P-01 blockDAG ordering fails

Fallback:

downgrade to linear PoW chain.

If P-04 light client privacy fails

Fallback:

narrow initial launch to stronger self-hosted or community-hosted privacy modes;

postpone broad mobile launch.

If P-05 relay privacy becomes too heavy

Fallback:

keep explicit degraded modes and ship stricter privacy messaging rather than pretending full anonymity.

If U-01 disclosure composition is too leaky

Fallback:

reduce proof types to payment proof, receipt proof, and invoice settlement proof only.

If U-02 stable layer becomes too centralized

Fallback:

postpone stable layer;

launch with base asset + fast payments only.

If X-01 recovery drifts toward custody

Fallback:

keep seed + threshold recovery only;

postpone social/institution-assisted recovery.

PoW base consensus

private-note model with commitments and nullifiers

strong full-node path

privacy-aware relay layer

base wallet + minimal merchant receipts

no stable layer unless it fully passes

no broad disclosure set, only core proof types

This is the architecture that should survive if ambition has to be cut.

B. Technical Whitepaper Draft
Title

A Layered Architecture for Private, Non-Custodial, Anti-Capture Digital Money

Abstract

This paper presents a formal architecture for a digital monetary system designed to maximize privacy, fungibility, decentralized validity, operational survivability, and narrow legal interoperability without default transparency. The design rejects the idea that one protocol layer can simultaneously solve sovereign settlement, everyday usability, price stability, business interoperability, and political resilience. Instead, it proposes a layered architecture composed of: a sovereign proof-of-work base with private transactions and public conflict markers; privacy-preserving transport and light-client mechanisms; upper layers for fast retail payments and a separate stable spending unit; scoped selective disclosure for business and legal workflows; bounded governance and multi-implementation discipline; and explicit kill criteria and launch gates. The paper’s thesis is that privacy-preserving money must be designed not only against cryptographic failure, but also against metadata leakage, product-layer betrayal, mining oligarchy, treasury capture, and governance drift.

1. Introduction

Digital money systems have historically optimized one property at the expense of others. Some prioritize transparency and auditability, sacrificing civil financial privacy. Others prioritize privacy on-chain while leaving network metadata, client behavior, and practical usability dangerously exposed. Some optimize throughput or application flexibility but depend on stake-weighted or validator-visible governance structures that are easier to capture socially and regulatorily. Others optimize everyday user convenience by reintroducing custodial trust or hidden intermediaries.

The architecture presented here starts from a harsher premise:

A serious digital monetary system must defend itself across multiple wars simultaneously:

the cryptographic war,

the metadata war,

the distribution and adoption war,

and the coercion war of banks, taxes, infrastructure, and law.

No single existing system closes all of those fronts. Current public systems already illustrate the fragmentation of the problem: IOTA Rebased moved toward a dPoS / MoveVM / staking model oriented to institutional interoperability and UX rather than hard anonymous cash; Monero remains one of the strongest public references for default on-chain privacy but documents practical caveats for lightweight wallet models using remote servers; Penumbra demonstrates a richer shielded-pool and selective-disclosure direction but remains a PoS system with bounded active validators and validator-visible governance; Cashu gives a compelling eCash UX, but its own documentation is explicit that the mint custodies underlying funds and can observe network metadata absent additional protections. FATF, meanwhile, continued tightening the global environment for Travel Rule / payment-transparency expectations in 2025.

The response in this paper is not to claim a single magical chain. It is to design a layered system with strict constitutional boundaries.

2. Problem statement

The target system is required, at the system level, to approximate the following properties:

privacy by default,

fungibility,

decentralized double-spend resistance,

usable daily payments,

compatibility with ordinary commercial life,

a route to relatively stable spending value,

survivability under regulatory and infrastructure pressure,

and bounded governance that cannot casually rewrite monetary rules.

These goals pull against each other.

A system that maximizes privacy but ignores light-client behavior and network metadata often becomes operationally transparent in practice.

A system that maximizes legal interoperability through public observability often becomes a financial surveillance rail.

A system that maximizes product convenience often quietly becomes custodial.

A system that maximizes stake-weighted or validator-friendly efficiency often becomes socially capturable.

A system that maximizes ideological “hardness” through zero long-term issuance often risks starving its security budget.

This paper therefore frames the problem not as “build another blockchain,” but as “build a constrained architecture whose layers each solve a bounded problem without corrupting the rest.”

3. Design principles

The architecture is governed by the following principles.

3.1 Base privacy must be default, not optional

If strong privacy is optional, the anonymity set fragments and the economic meaning of fungibility degrades.

3.2 Validity must not derive from prior wealth

The base layer rejects stake-derived sovereignty and visible-validator rule as the foundation of settlement validity.

3.3 Network metadata is part of monetary privacy

Ledger privacy without transport privacy is incomplete.

3.4 Light clients must not be privacy theater

If mobile or lightweight use requires handing remote providers a clean wallet-activity graph, the architecture has failed socially even if its cryptography is sound.

3.5 Stable value must be layered, not fused into sovereignty

The stable spending unit must not become the hidden central bank of the entire system.

3.6 Legal interoperability must be narrow and scoped

The architecture supports selective disclosure, not universal transparency.

3.7 Governance must exist but remain bounded

The protocol must evolve without turning into a foundation-state or dev oligarchy.

3.8 Complexity must justify itself

Every subsystem must beat the simpler fallback not only in technical cleverness, but in total system fitness.

4. System overview

The architecture is composed of five main layers.

Layer 0 — Privacy transport network

A privacy-aware relay and propagation layer designed to reduce transaction-origin inference, wallet clustering, and metadata exposure.

Layer 1 — Sovereign monetary base

A proof-of-work base ledger with private-note transactions, commitments, nullifiers, aggregate supply correctness, and non-custodial double-spend prevention.

Layer 2 — Fast payments layer

A bounded-exposure layer for low-friction daily payments, conceptually inspired by eCash or similar fast-spending abstractions, but explicitly not the sovereign settlement base.

Layer 3 — Stable spending unit

A separate upper-layer monetary abstraction intended for pricing, payroll, recurring payments, and routine commerce, with explicit collateral or reserve rules and failure isolation.

Layer 4 — Selective disclosure layer

A scoped-proof layer for receipts, payroll, invoice settlement, reserve snapshots, audit slices, and other narrow commercial/legal evidence.

These layers are surrounded by:

a bounded governance and upgrade architecture,

a multi-implementation strategy,

and explicit kill criteria and launch gates.

5. Sovereign base-layer architecture
5.1 Consensus

The base layer uses a proof-of-work family as its security anchor. This is not because PoW is considered perfect; it is chosen provisionally because it anchors history rewriting to external cost instead of converting prior wealth into protocol authority.

The preferred ledger target is a PoW blockDAG with deterministic canonical ordering and private-spend conflict resolution via public nullifiers. However, this preference is explicitly conditional. If ordering complexity, light-client burden, or state-update ambiguity prove too high, the architecture must fall back to a linear PoW chain rather than ship a clever failure.

5.2 Private transaction model

The base asset uses a private note model rather than transparent account balances.

Each spendable unit is represented by a hidden note committed to the chain. Transactions create new output commitments, reveal nullifiers for spent notes, and include proofs that:

the spender is authorized,

the inputs existed,

values are conserved under hidden arithmetic,

and no unauthorized issuance occurred.

5.3 Supply correctness

Although individual amounts and flows are hidden, aggregate supply must remain publicly auditable. This is achieved through:

proof-enforced hidden-value conservation,

explicit issuance paths,

explicit burn paths,

and publicly derivable aggregate issuance logic.

5.4 State commitments

Canonical chain state commits to:

note commitment state,

nullifier state,

and optionally auxiliary accounting roots.

Only canonicalized order updates state. Provisional branches are observable but not final.

6. Mining and issuance

The base security model uses a memory-hard, commodity-hardware-biased PoW family as the preferred direction. The goal is not to make specialization impossible forever, but to avoid immediate surrender to ASIC-maximalist industrial oligopoly.

The monetary policy rejects both large premine aristocracy and hard-zero issuance absolutism. The preferred issuance philosophy is:

broad fair-launch acquisition over time,

no large insider allocation,

possibly a small tightly bounded bootstrap reserve,

emission that declines over time,

and a persistent low security tail to maintain miner incentives.

This choice is not aesthetic. It is driven by the claim that a privacy-heavy sovereign base cannot safely assume transaction fees alone will always fund honest security.

7. Light clients and state access

A private system fails socially if only full-node purists can use it.

At the same time, it fails architecturally if light clients must blindly trust remote indexers or leak their wallet activity through naive scanning.

The architecture therefore requires:

full nodes as the source of truth,

light clients that verify meaningful state locally,

untrusted remote providers treated as transport rather than truth,

multi-provider synchronization,

and a note-discovery model that remains recipient-possible, provider-ambiguous, and locally verifiable.

This remains one of the most difficult unresolved operational areas and is treated as a first-class blocker.

8. Network privacy

The system rejects plain-origin gossip as the default broadcast model.

Instead, it targets a staged propagation model in which transactions first move through a privacy-aware relay path before broader diffusion. The goals are:

source ambiguity,

timing de-correlation,

spy-node resistance,

eclipse resistance,

and viable mobile operation under constrained conditions.

The architecture distinguishes explicitly between:

strong privacy mode,

reduced privacy mode,

and emergency-connectivity mode.

The product must surface those differences honestly to users.

9. Fast payments and productization

Daily payments should not require base-layer settlement for every interaction.

The architecture therefore allows an upper-layer fast-payments model with bounded exposure. This layer is conceptually compatible with eCash-like or similar structures, but the architecture insists that any such layer must:

remain subordinate to the base asset,

have bounded blast radius,

and never be mistaken for sovereign settlement.

The product layer must distinguish clearly between:

base asset,

fast-payment balance,

and stable spending unit.

Wallet UX is governed by an “honest interface” rule:
the application must tell the truth about privacy mode, settlement finality, trust boundaries, and recovery limits. Convenience may not be purchased by silent custody or silent metadata leakage.

10. Selective disclosure and business interoperability

The architecture supports business operations through scoped, minimally sufficient proofs rather than universal transparency.

Supported proof types include:

payment proof,

receipt proof,

invoice-settlement proof,

payroll proof,

reserve or solvency proof,

audit-slice proof,

and dispute proof.

These proofs must be:

purpose-scoped,

time-scoped,

audience-scoped,

and narrow enough that ordinary commercial use does not require exposing the user’s full financial graph.

The chain itself is not the accounting system.
It provides verifiable settlement facts; accounting remains an upper-layer local record system.

11. Stable spending unit

The architecture rejects the idea that the sovereign base asset should also be the sole stable everyday spending unit.

Instead, it defines a separate stable spending layer with hard boundaries:

the stable unit may be overcollateralized or otherwise explicitly backed,

its treasury and collateral logic must remain separate from base consensus,

its failure must be locally bounded,

and depeg states must be treated as explicit protocol states rather than PR accidents.

Aggregate accountability is required. Universal user-level transparency is not.

If a stable-layer design can only function by installing a hidden central bank, a central issuer chokepoint, or a treasury that politically dominates the protocol, it fails this architecture.

12. Governance and evolution

The architecture accepts that some governance is unavoidable, but rejects discretionary monetary management.

Changes are classified into:

non-consensus changes,

compatibility-preserving technical changes,

consensus-rule changes,

constitutional changes,

and emergencies.

Emergency coordination may exist, but it is strictly bounded. It may:

publish advisories,

coordinate patches,

and classify incidents,
but it may not become an indefinite sovereign office.

The protocol itself is defined by:

specifications,

conformance behavior,

and multiple independent implementations,
not by a single reference codebase.

13. Product and business interface

The architecture supports four broad wallet profiles:

sovereign wallet,

everyday wallet,

business wallet,

and watch/audit wallet.

Merchant flows include:

invoices,

receipts,

refunds,

recurring payments,

and dispute bundles.

Recovery is supported through multiple models such as self-custody, threshold recovery, and social recovery, but the product must never hide when “recovery help” is actually delegated control.

The narrowest adequate disclosure must be the easiest path in the interface.

14. Failure model

The architecture distinguishes:

local failures,

structural failures,

and constitutional failures.

Examples of constitutional failure include:

practical privacy impossible under usable light-client conditions,

supply correctness not independently auditable,

stable-layer logic becoming the real sovereign center,

or ordinary business usability only becoming viable through broad transparency.

The system is required to define not only launch gates, but also kill criteria and downgrade paths. Examples:

downgrade blockDAG to a linear chain if complexity becomes unreviewable;

postpone the stable layer if it centralizes too much;

narrow disclosure scope if proof composition becomes too linkable;

cut recovery features if they drift toward hidden custody.

The architecture prefers the narrowest viable surviving system over the richest speculative system.

15. Validation and launch discipline

No mainnet launch is acceptable unless all mandatory gates pass for included layers.

These gates include:

consensus correctness,

private-state correctness,

light-client viability,

network privacy viability,

mining and issuance viability,

governance and multi-implementation viability,

product usability,

disclosure safety,

and stable-layer resilience if the stable unit launches with the system.

Testnets must be staged:

cryptography and state,

consensus and reorg behavior,

light clients and network privacy,

product and merchant flows,

and integrated adversarial testing.

16. Comparison to existing public systems

This architecture does not claim that current systems are worthless. It treats them as sources of partial lessons.

IOTA Rebased shows how a modern high-performance, institution-friendly system can converge on dPoS, staking, validators, and identity-adjacent product design; for this architecture that serves more as a warning about base-layer sovereignty boundaries than as a template.

Monero shows why privacy by default and fungibility matter, but its own wallet documentation also illustrates how remote lightweight operation can create practical privacy weaknesses.

Penumbra shows a strong direction for shielded-pool logic and selective disclosure but remains anchored to PoS and validator-visible structures, which this architecture rejects at the base sovereignty layer.

Cashu shows why everyday digital cash UX matters, while simultaneously illustrating why fast payments and sovereign settlement must not be conflated.

The point is not to copy one of them.
It is to extract the least self-defeating lessons from all of them.

17. Open problems

The architecture remains incomplete until the following are closed:

exact PoW function and parameterization,

exact blockDAG ordering rule,

exact hidden-value proof system,

exact note-discovery and light-client query protocol,

exact relay/mixnet design,

exact stable-unit collateral and oracle-minimization design,

exact tail-emission formula,

and exact activation thresholds for change classes.

These are open by design, not by omission.

18. Conclusion

This paper proposes a layered monetary architecture built on a simple but demanding premise:

No single protocol layer can honestly deliver privacy, sovereignty, usability, stability, legal interoperability, and anti-capture resilience all at once.

So the system must be built as a hierarchy of constrained layers:

a private, non-custodial, proof-of-work sovereign base;

privacy-aware transport and viable light clients;

bounded fast-payments abstractions;

a separate stable spending unit;

scoped disclosure for commerce and law;

and bounded governance with explicit kill criteria.

The central thesis is not that this architecture is easy.
It is that any architecture that pretends these tensions do not exist is already lying.

B3. Glossary of unified terms

To keep the architecture coherent, use these terms consistently:

Base asset: the sovereign privacy-preserving settlement asset

Stable unit: upper-layer stable spending denomination

Fast layer: bounded-exposure everyday payments layer

Note: private spendable state object

Commitment: public cryptographic commitment to hidden note state

Nullifier: public one-time conflict marker used to prevent double spend

Canonical order: definitive block / transaction ordering used for state updates

Selective disclosure object: scoped verifiable proof for a narrow fact

Strong privacy mode: healthy metadata-protective networking posture

Reduced privacy mode: functionally usable but weaker metadata posture

Emergency connectivity mode: degraded network-privacy posture explicitly surfaced to the user

Constitutional invariant: rule not subject to routine governance

Kill criterion: condition under which the architecture must stop or reset

Downgrade path: deliberate simplification route when ambition outruns viability

D4. Private transaction lifecycle
User note(s)
   │
   ▼
Select spendable note commitments
   │
   ▼
Derive spend authorization + nullifier(s)
   │
   ▼
Construct outputs (new hidden notes)
   │
   ▼

- inputs exist
- spender authorized
- values conserved
- nullifiers valid
- issuance rules obeyed
   │
   ▼
Send transaction into privacy relay path
   │
   ▼
Transaction enters consensus frontier
   │
   ▼
Canonical ordering resolves conflicts
   │
   ▼
Accepted transaction updates:
- note commitment state
- nullifier state
- aggregate fee / issuance accounting

providers may serve data,

they do not define truth,

the wallet must not reveal its full note graph to one provider.

   │
   ├─ local failure? ─────────────► replace component
   │
   ├─ structural failure? ────────► simplify architecture
   │                                examples:
   │                                - blockDAG → linear chain
   │                                - stable layer postponed
   │                                - disclosure scope reduced
   │
   └─ constitutional failure? ────► stop / restart architecture
                                    examples:
                                    - no viable private light client
                                    - unauditable hidden inflation risk
                                    - business use requires transparency
                                    - practical path becomes custodial
2. Formal Research Backlog

Below is the execution backlog in research-program form.

Each item has:

Question

Why it matters

Tasks

Deliverables

Acceptance

Kill / downgrade trigger

Dependencies

Priority

RB-01 — Canonical ordering for PoW blockDAG

Question
Can a PoW blockDAG produce deterministic canonical ordering that remains understandable, conflict-safe, and independently implementable?

Why it matters
If not, the base ledger must downgrade to a linear chain.

Tasks

define candidate ordering rules;

model parallel honest block production;

simulate double-spend conflicts with hidden transactions;

produce state-transition fixtures for reorg scenarios;

compare complexity against linear-chain fallback.

Deliverables

ordering algorithm memo;

simulation report;

conformance vectors;

downgrade recommendation.

Acceptance

deterministic order across independent implementations;

no ambiguity for nullifier conflicts;

comprehensible wallet-facing finality model.

Kill / downgrade

downgrade to linear chain if ordering remains too complex or brittle.

Dependencies

C-01, C-02

Priority

P0

RB-02 — PoW function selection

Question
Which memory-hard, commodity-accessible PoW family best delays oligopoly without becoming operationally naive?

Why it matters
PoW choice decides who can mine, how fast pools dominate, and how expensive capture becomes.

Tasks

compare candidate memory-hard PoW families;

model CPU / GPU / ASIC pressure;

model cloud-rental and botnet abuse;

estimate verification cost for full nodes;

study interaction with proof-heavy private transactions.

Deliverables

PoW candidate comparison;

anti-centralization risk report;

recommended parameter set.

Acceptance

verification remains cheap;

no immediate hardware aristocracy;

attack-cost model is defensible.

Kill / downgrade

if no candidate avoids rapid unacceptable concentration, revisit consensus assumptions or accept narrower ambition.

Dependencies

RB-01, P-02

Priority

P0

RB-03 — Private-note proof system

Question
What proof system should enforce ownership, nullifier correctness, conservation, and range validity without making verification or implementation unmanageable?

Why it matters
This is the monetary integrity core.

Tasks

compare proof-system families;

define proof statements needed by the architecture;

benchmark prover and verifier costs;

model upgrade path for proof migration;

review soundness and implementation burden.

Deliverables

proof-system decision memo;

benchmark suite;

threat analysis.

Acceptance

sound hidden-value conservation;

practical full-node verification;

realistic future migration path.

Kill / downgrade

if proof burden is too heavy or auditability too weak, simplify privacy model before launch.

Dependencies

RB-01

Priority

P0

RB-04 — Commitment and state-root design

Question
What commitment / accumulator structure best supports private notes, nullifiers, reorgs, and light clients?

Why it matters
State commitments are where consensus, proofs, and wallets all meet.

Tasks

compare tree / accumulator families;

define note-state and nullifier-state separation;

test root determinism across implementations;

model reorg updates;

quantify proof sizes.

Deliverables

state-structure spec;

root determinism test harness;

reorg update model.

Acceptance

deterministic roots;

practical proof sizes;

manageable implementation surface.

Kill / downgrade

if state logic becomes too complex under blockDAG, support linear fallback.

Dependencies

RB-01, RB-03

Priority

P0

RB-05 — Light-client privacy and note discovery

Question
Can the system support a mobile light client that does not leak near-complete wallet activity?

Why it matters
This is one of the top constitutional blockers.

Tasks

define candidate note-discovery mechanisms;

model remote candidate retrieval privacy;

test multi-provider sync;

benchmark mobile bandwidth, battery, and latency;

quantify what one provider, two colluding providers, and relay+provider can infer.

Deliverables

light-client architecture report;

privacy leakage measurement;

mobile prototype.

Acceptance

usable mobile sync;

local verification of meaningful proofs;

no catastrophic provider-side wallet reconstruction.

Kill / downgrade

constitutional failure if only operationally suicidal light clients are possible.

Dependencies

RB-03, RB-04, RB-07

Priority

P0

RB-06 — Aggregate supply correctness audit

Question
Can independent teams verify aggregate issuance and hidden-value correctness with high confidence?

Why it matters
Without this, private money becomes unauditable black-box money.

Tasks

formalize issuance accounting;

formalize fee accounting;

test hidden-value conservation across implementations;

create inflation-bug detection procedures;

define anomaly investigation flow.

Deliverables

aggregate supply audit method;

conformance vectors;

emergency inflation response playbook.

Acceptance

independent audit reproducibility;

no dependence on one client or one team.

Kill / downgrade

constitutional failure if aggregate correctness cannot be audited.

Dependencies

RB-03, RB-04

Priority

P0

RB-07 — Network relay / mix privacy

Question
What relay model best reduces transaction-origin inference without destroying usability?

Why it matters
Ledger privacy dies if transport privacy is weak.

Tasks

compare staged relay, Dandelion-like, and mixnet-like approaches;

simulate spy-node observation;

simulate origin inference;

benchmark latency under mobile conditions;

define degraded-mode thresholds.

Deliverables

network privacy design report;

attack simulation results;

mobile networking profile.

Acceptance

materially reduced origin inference;

usable latency;

explicit degraded mode behavior.

Kill / downgrade

if relay privacy is too unusable, ship with honest weaker modes, not fake strong claims.

Dependencies

RB-05 partially, X-01 partially

Priority

P0

RB-08 — Mining concentration and pool behavior

Question
How quickly does mining centralize under the chosen PoW and issuance model?

Why it matters
A sovereign base that centralizes immediately is self-defeating.

Tasks

simulate miner economics under multiple hardware classes;

model pool incentives;

model censorship and withholding behavior;

track hosted-infrastructure dependency;

define monitoring metrics.

Deliverables

mining concentration report;

pool-risk model;

anti-centralization monitoring plan.

Acceptance

plausible open participation window;

no immediate de facto oligarchy.

Kill / downgrade

if security centralizes too early, revisit PoW and issuance.

Dependencies

RB-02, RB-11

Priority

P1

RB-09 — Selective disclosure composition safety

Question
Can narrow proofs be used in business and legal workflows without trivially reconstructing the user’s full graph when combined?

Why it matters
Selective disclosure is only real if repeated disclosures do not collapse into broad traceability.

Tasks

define proof classes;

simulate common proof combinations;

design time-scope and audience-scope boundaries;

test oversharing-prone UX flows;

formalize proof minimization hierarchy.

Deliverables

disclosure composition report;

safe proof catalog;

UI red-line guide.

Acceptance

common business proofs remain narrow;

composition risk bounded and explainable.

Kill / downgrade

if narrow proofs naturally reconstruct broad history, reduce proof surface.

Dependencies

RB-03, RB-04, RB-15

Priority

P1

RB-10 — Merchant and business operations model

Question
Can merchants, payroll operators, and businesses use the system without broad transparency or hidden custody?

Why it matters
This decides whether the architecture is economically usable.

Tasks

prototype invoice flow;

prototype receipt and refund flows;

prototype payroll proofs;

define local accounting export boundaries;

run business workflow tests.

Deliverables

merchant flow spec;

business wallet workflow pack;

accounting export format.

Acceptance

invoices, receipts, refunds, and payroll all work with narrow proofs.

Kill / downgrade

if businesses can only operate through full-history exports, the design fails constitutionally.

Dependencies

RB-05, RB-09, RB-15

Priority

P1

RB-11 — Issuance curve and security budget

Question
What emission schedule keeps security alive without baking in oligarchy or arbitrary inflation?

Why it matters
This determines long-term survival.

Tasks

model fair-launch acquisition under realistic miner behavior;

compare emission curves;

model fee volatility;

test tail-emission-like scenarios;

bound bootstrap reserve size and powers.

Deliverables

monetary policy report;

emission curve candidate set;

reserve boundary spec.

Acceptance

legible policy;

persistent security budget;

no structural insider domination.

Kill / downgrade

if security depends on speculative fee optimism or insider concentration, redesign issuance.

Dependencies

RB-02, RB-08

Priority

P1

RB-12 — Stable spending unit architecture

Question
Can a stable unit exist without becoming a hidden central bank or requiring transparency-by-default?

Why it matters
This decides whether the system can support everyday pricing at scale.

Tasks

compare overcollateralized models;

model collateral crash and liquidation paths;

model oracle minimization;

define redemption modes and depeg states;

test stable/base isolation.

Deliverables

stable-unit architecture report;

stress test suite;

depeg state machine.

Acceptance

failure remains local;

aggregate solvency is legible;

treasury cannot dominate base consensus.

Kill / downgrade

if stability only works via central issuer or hidden treasury sovereignty, postpone stable layer.

Dependencies

RB-03, RB-11, RB-14

Priority

P2

RB-13 — Governance and emergency control design

Question
Can the protocol evolve and respond to crises without becoming a technocracy?

Why it matters
Most systems get captured here, not in the whitepaper.

Tasks

define change classes;

design activation logic;

design emergency categories;

design emergency sunset rules;

create multi-implementation conformance process.

Deliverables

governance charter draft;

emergency playbooks;

conformance governance model.

Acceptance

no routine discretionary control over constitutional rules;

no single-client protocol monopoly.

Kill / downgrade

if emergency authority becomes permanent or one implementation defines truth, redesign governance.

Dependencies

all core protocol specs

Priority

P2

RB-14 — Stable/base political boundary

Question
Can stable-layer treasury and business pressure be prevented from capturing base-layer evolution?

Why it matters
Even a technically separate stable layer can become the real sovereign center.

Tasks

model treasury influence scenarios;

design formal hard boundaries;

stress-test governance spillover;

define forbidden powers;

map crisis communications between layers.

Deliverables

treasury-boundary charter;

capture analysis memo.

Acceptance

base rules remain independent even under stable-layer distress.

Kill / downgrade

if the stable unit becomes the cultural or technical control plane, reduce or postpone it.

RB-12, RB-13

Priority

P2

RB-15 — Wallet UX, recovery, and operational honesty

Question
Can ordinary users use the system without constant fatal mistakes and without being misled about trust, privacy, or finality?

Why it matters
This is where strong architectures die socially.

Tasks

prototype wallet information architecture;

define privacy-mode indicators;

define finality-state UI;

test recovery comprehension;

test oversharing-prevention UX.

Deliverables

wallet UX spec;

recovery policy templates;

merchant UX pack;

privacy-state labeling guide.

Acceptance

users can distinguish layers, privacy modes, and finality states;

recovery help is not confused with custody.

Kill / downgrade

if the only practical UX is effectively custodial, constitutional failure.

RB-05, RB-07, RB-09, RB-10

Priority

P1

RB-16 — Integrated adversarial testnet

Question
Do the subsystems survive when combined under hostile conditions?

Why it matters
Subsystem success alone is not enough.

Tasks

build integrated testnet;

include hostile miners, hostile providers, hostile relays, hostile merchants;

run state-root divergence drills;

run deanonymization drills;

run emergency upgrade drills.

Deliverables

integrated adversarial testnet;

launch-gate report;

downgrade recommendations.

Acceptance

no constitutional failures under integrated testing;

unresolved structural failures have explicit simplification paths.

Kill / downgrade

if integrated behavior breaks key invariants, stop or simplify before launch.
